                .addSize([320, 100], [[300, 50], [320, 50]])

        gptPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.page ? digitalData.page.page : "blank";
        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

        googletag.pubads()
            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/smll.202403736')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="bc21f41e18ab4483a3dc89ed3039aa3d"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="bc21f41e18ab4483a3dc89ed3039aa3d">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'e6f1ae85-1f8f-4f85-8a7e-96f512d5ecb7'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        (function() {
            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {
                var store_domain = 'https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com';
                var session_id = '572752225B72D785F73FA43CF5A65E11';
                var url = ('https:' == document.location.protocol ? 'https://' : 'http://')
                    + "beacon.riskified.com?shop=" + store_domain + "&sid=" + session_id;
                var s = document.createElement('script');
                s.type = 'text/javascript';
                s.async = true;
                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }
            if (window.attachEvent)
                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)
            else
                window.addEventListener('load', riskifiedBeaconLoad, false);
        })();

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d89545374" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Transition metal selenides (TMSs) are receiving considerable interest as improved anode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) and lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) due to their considerable theoretical capacity and excellent redox reversibility. Herein, ZIF-12 (zeolitic imidazolate framework) structure is used for the synthesis of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC anode material by pyrolysis of ZIF-12/Se mixture. When Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC composite is utilized as an anode electrode material in LIB and SIB half cells, the material demonstrates excellent electrochemical performance and remarkable cycle stability with retaining high capacities. In LIB and SIB half cells, the Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC anode material shows the ultralong lifespan at 2000 mAg<sup>−1</sup>, retaining a capacity of 543 mAhg<sup>−1</sup> after 750 cycles, and retaining a capacity of 251 mAhg<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles at 100 mAg<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The porous structure of the Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC anode material can not only effectively tolerate the volume expansion of the electrode during discharging and charging, but also facilitate the penetration of electrolyte and efficiently prevents the clustering of active particles. In situ X-ray difraction (XRD) analysis results reveal the high potential of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC composite in building efficient LIBs and SIBs due to reversible conversion reactions of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC for lithium-ion and sodium-ion storage.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202403736-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202403736-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>It is essential to prioritize the creation of eco-friendly energy storage and utilization technologies due to escalating air pollution.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0001_R_d89545366e456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Inorg. Chem. Front.">1</a>-<a href="#smll202403736-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0003_R_d89545366e456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Coord. Chem. Rev.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, there is a growing demand for advanced power storage systems due to the growth of many electric and hybrid vehicles, as well as large-scale industrial power and energy.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0004_R_d89545366e461" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Chem. Eng. J.">4</a>-<a href="#smll202403736-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0006_R_d89545366e461" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - ChemBioEng Rev.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Li-ion batteries (LIBs) are considered the most efficient electrochemical energy conversion technology due to their remarkable attributes of high energy densities and exceptional stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0007_R_d89545366e466" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Energy">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Recently, a significant increase in attention has been directed toward SIBs because of the plentiful availability of sodium and comparatively lower cost compared to LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0008_R_d89545366e472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> SIBs are limited by slow reaction rates and little capacity for storing sodium ions. Current LIBs and SIBs are insufficient in serving the increasing requirements of extensive applications, such as electric cars, hybrid vehicles, and future smart grids.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0009_R_d89545366e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Joule">9</a>, <a href="#smll202403736-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0010_R_d89545366e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Chem. Rec.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Graphite as anode electrode material has an insufficient rate capability and fails to meet the requirements of extensive energy applications despite possessing several advantageous characteristics, such as exceptional cycling stability, high Coulombic efficiency (CE), and abundant availability in nature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0011_R_d89545366e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Sci.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Theconventional graphite with a well-organized structure as anode material used in SIBs is not well-suited to accommodate Na<sup>+</sup> due to the challenges associated with the creation of intercalation compounds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0012_R_d89545366e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, a considerable amount of research has been dedicated to investigating battery materials with the aim of achieving enhanced performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0013_R_d89545366e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Energy Chem.">13</a>-<a href="#smll202403736-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0018_R_d89545366e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Recently, researchers have been focusing on systematically studying transition-metal chalcogenides (S, Se, and Te) as possible anode materials for batteries. This interest stems from their significant theoretical capabilities and favourable chemical stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0019_R_d89545366e503" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Rare Met.">19</a>-<a href="#smll202403736-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0021_R_d89545366e503" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Small">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the selenium (Se) element has viable redox characteristics, hence facilitating exceptional transport of ions capacity and cycle stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0022_R_d89545366e508" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> TMSs are being used more frequently as anode materials and have become a topic of growing interest in recent years. CoSe<sub>2</sub>, Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>, Cu<sub>2</sub>Se, ZnSe, and FeSe are noteworthy for their elevated theoretical capacity. For example, the theoretical capacities of Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub> and Cu<sub>2</sub>Se for SIBs batteries were computed as 435 and 260 mAhg<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. These materials are also cost-effective and exhibit enhanced electrical conductivity when compared to oxides or sulfides.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0023_R_d89545366e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Small">23</a>-<a href="#smll202403736-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0025_R_d89545366e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Energy Storage Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the incorporation of an additional metallic component has the potential to more effectively manage the electrical configuration of transition metal semiconductors, augment conductivity via coupling and synergistic phenomena, and generate diverse phase interfaces and structural imperfections. Bimetallic selenides can exhibit enhanced electrochemical activity due to the presence of several metal cations with distinct oxidation states, which allows for numerous redox processes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0020_R_d89545366e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Mater. Chem. A">20</a>, <a href="#smll202403736-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0026_R_d89545366e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Power Sources">26</a>, <a href="#smll202403736-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0027_R_d89545366e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Nanoscale">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, transition metal semiconductors continue to have various limitations, including significant volume fluctuations during the periodic charging and discharging processes, as well as reduced conductivity. These shortcomings result in poor cycle and rate performance, particularly when exposed to high current densities.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0020_R_d89545366e542" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Mater. Chem. A">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, in conjunction with the design of the structure, the application of a carbon layer over the hollow structure may serve as a potential approach to mitigate electrode aggregation and collapse, as well as to reduce volume fluctuations when inserting and extracting Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0028_R_d89545366e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Chem. Rev.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Over the course of the previous decade, metal-organic frameworks (MOFs) have been employed as efficient materials to create transition metal systems characterized with distinct shapes, such as hollow spheres. These transition metal systems exhibit advantageous properties such as elevated specific surface areas and consistent microporous architectures. Utilization of the MOFs template methodology for the fabrication of two-dimensional structured TMSs is expected to be appealing.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0029_R_d89545366e558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Coord. Chem. Rev.">29</a>-<a href="#smll202403736-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0031_R_d89545366e558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Electrochem. Energy Rev.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While it is possible to directly produce hollow TMS and N-doped carbon composites from MOFs, there is still scope to improve the percentage ratios of carbon and nitrogen elements, in addition to the carbon conductive skeleton structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0032_R_d89545366e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Ceram. Int.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The materials derived from MOFs possess active sites that are prominently exposed, allowing for the integration of the high electrical conductivity of porous carbons with the electrochemical capabilities of other derived materials, including metal compounds, doped metals, and heteroatoms. This combination optimizes the rate and capacity performance of these materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0033_R_d89545366e570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Nanoscale">33</a>-<a href="#smll202403736-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0035_R_d89545366e570" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Mater. Chem. A">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Researchers frequently utilize nanostructure design and carbon modification as common approaches to addressing the issues associated with anode materials in TMSe.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0036_R_d89545366e575" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - ChemPlusChem">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, it has been observed that MOF-based bimetallic selenides have enhanced electrochemical performance in comparison to selenides composed of a single metal.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0037_R_d89545366e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">37</a>-<a href="#smll202403736-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0039_R_d89545366e580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Nanoscale">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Wang et&nbsp;al. introduced a Zn/Co/Se@NCP nanomaterial derived from ZIF-8@ZIF-67 precursor for sodium<sup>-</sup>ion batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0040_R_d89545366e588" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Zhu et&nbsp;al. prepared a Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Co<sub>0.67</sub>Se hollow nanoprisms, which showed a good discharge capacity for LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0041_R_d89545366e598" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Nano Res.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Fang et&nbsp;al. reported Cu-doped CoSe<sub>2</sub> microboxes with high conductivity and a capacity of 492 mAhg<sup>−1</sup> at 1.0 Ag<sup>−1</sup> for SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0042_R_d89545366e610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Mater.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Yan et&nbsp;al. synthesized ZnSe<sub>2</sub>/(CoSe<sub>2</sub>)@NC nanoparticles, and it shows a high reversible capacity of 552 mAhg<sup>−1</sup> at 100 mAg<sup>−1</sup> after 100 cycles with demonstrated outstanding cycling stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0043_R_d89545366e624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Composites Part B">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Herein, we have successfully produced bimetallic cobalt-copper selenide nanomaterials, namely Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC embedded in a NPC matrix. Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC material was achieved for the first time by employing the Cu@ZIF-12. Metal compounds based on Cu and Co can improve the storage behavior of lithium and sodium ions by combining the benefits of two metallic compounds, resulting in increased capacity. The carbon frameworks have the capacity to efficiently mitigate the volume fluctuations that occur during charge/discharge cycles in Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles, hence improving the stability of their structure. Furthermore, the addition of defect-rich N-doped amorphous carbon has the potential to improve electrical conductivity and create active sites for the absorption of Li/Na ions on the surface. This, in addition, can contribute to the overall capacity by increasing the surface capacitance under high-rate conditions. Therefore, through the interactions and combined influence of different TMSs with a NPC lead to desirable specific capacity, remarkable rate performance, and cycle stability for SIBs as well as LIBs. To explain outstanding electrochemical performance of the Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC in SIB and LIB half battery cells, the Li and Na reaction mechanisms of the Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC composite were analyzed by in situ XRD analysis.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202403736-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202403736-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202403736-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202403736-sec-0030-title">2.1 The Morphology and Structural Characterization of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC</h3>
            
            <p>The Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC synthesis involves a two-step method, illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202403736-fig-0001">1</a>. First, Cu@ZIF-12 precursor has been obtained by a solvothermal method. Second, anode materials were made by combining with selenium powder and undergoing selenization and carbonization at 800&nbsp;°C for 2 h in an argon environment. In the subsequent selenization process, the Co and Cu nanoparticles that are embedded in the ZIF structure react with Se and are converted to Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC nanoparticles. The detailed experiments are provided in the Supporting Information.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202403736-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/891c9713-02d5-468c-9a8a-b60e89f7d8ee/smll202403736-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/891c9713-02d5-468c-9a8a-b60e89f7d8ee/smll202403736-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f83413eb-a4a8-461e-bcd7-a004e745971f/smll202403736-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/891c9713-02d5-468c-9a8a-b60e89f7d8ee/smll202403736-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202403736-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202403736" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic diagram formation mechanism of the anode material from ZIF-12.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To verify the specific crystallinity of the materials, XRD analysis was performed (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202403736-fig-0002">2</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). The XRD patterns of ZIF-12 and Cu@ZIF-12 are presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403736-fig-0002">2a</a> and shows characteristic diffraction peaks at 4.34, 6.12, 7.52, 12.32, 15.72, 16.90, and 18.50° attributed to the (011), (002), (112), (004), (015), (044), and (006) lattice planes of ZIF-12.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0044_R_d89545366e735" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As expected, a slight peak shift was observed for Cu-ZIF-12 in comparison with the data of ZIF-12 due to the incorporation of Cu into ZIF-12 lattice planes. After selenization, Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub> (COD no. 9015293 and 9012804) compound is mainly detected. To examine the appropriate synthesis process of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC, XRD analysis is performed on Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>, as represented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403736-fig-0002">2b</a>. It is found that Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub> peaks located at 17.03, 26.35, 29.93, 33.47, 34.46, 45.61, 47.48, 51.45, 62.46, and 71.56° corresponding to (200), (110), (−401), (002), (400), (401), (−512), (−313), (022), and (−114) lattice planes, respectively, as indexed to the standard COD no: 9012804, revealing that the sample has monoclinic unit cell. It is included in the C12/m1 (12) space group with a lattice parameter a = 11.93 Å, b = 3.57 Å, and c = 6.14 Å.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0045_R_d89545366e768" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Solid State Sci.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In Cu<sub>2</sub>Se, the acquired XRD peaks are located at 26.65, 44.23, and 52.39° corresponding to (111), (022), and (131) lattice planes, respectively, which have been assigned as the primary peaks to guarantee the accurate synthesis of cubic Cu<sub>2</sub>Se with a COD no: 9015293. It is included in the Fm-3m (225) space group with a lattice parameter a = 5.78 Å, b = 5.78 Å, and c = 5.78 Å.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0046_R_d89545366e778" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - J. Solid State Chem.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information) present the Rietveld refinement result of the Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC and XRD analyses of Co@NPC and Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC materials, respectively.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202403736-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/20281649-4e13-4d6d-a623-3dfe6f3a33b2/smll202403736-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/20281649-4e13-4d6d-a623-3dfe6f3a33b2/smll202403736-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/419a36ee-1ec6-407e-b215-9b98b73c2438/smll202403736-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/20281649-4e13-4d6d-a623-3dfe6f3a33b2/smll202403736-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202403736-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202403736" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">XRD patterns of ZIF-12, Cu@ZIF-12 a), Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC b), XPS survey spectrum of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC c), high-resolution spectra of Cu 2p d), Co 2p e), O 1s f), N 1s g), C 1s h), and Se 3d 1).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) analysis is utilized to determine the surface electronic states and chemical compositions of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC. The survey spectra depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403736-fig-0002">2c</a> indicates that Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC comprises Co, Cu, Se, O, C, and N the elements. The presence of oxygen can be attributed to the sensitivity of selenide surface to oxygen.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0047_R_d89545366e852" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403736-fig-0002">2d–i</a> displays the high-resolution XPS spectra of Cu 2p, Co 2p, O 1s, N 1s, C 1s, and Se 3d. The presence of Cu(I) and Cu(II) in Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC is confirmed by the distinct peaks at 932.3 and 951.7&nbsp;eV, corresponding to Cu(I) 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Cu(I) 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, and signals at 933.8 and 953&nbsp;eV attributed to Cu(II) 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Cu(II) 2p<sub>1/2</sub> (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202403736-fig-0003">3d</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0048_R_d89545366e882" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - ACS Nano">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403736-fig-0003">3e</a> shows the Co 2p spectra with peaks at 780.5&nbsp;eV belonging to Co 2p<sub>3/2</sub> of Co<sup>3+</sup> and 796.3&nbsp;eV attributed to Co 2p<sub>1/2</sub> of Co<sup>3+</sup>. These peaks likely result from the limited oxidation of the surface of CoSe<sub>2</sub>. The peak energy at 782.4 corresponds to the Co 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 798.7&nbsp;eV attributed to Co 2p<sub>1/2</sub> of Co<sup>2+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0049_R_d89545366e908" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - J. Mater. Chem. A">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403736-fig-0003">3f</a>, the signals at 529.9 (C═O), 530.9 (C─O-Metal), and 532.7 (C─O bond) were observed. The signal at 535.0&nbsp;eV is attributed to absorbed O<sub>2</sub> from atmosphere. The peaks at 398.1, 399.8, 401.0, and 405.6&nbsp;eV attributed to pyridinic-N, pyrrolic-N, graphitic-N, and oxidized-N (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403736-fig-0003">3g</a>). The peaks at 284.4, 285.8, and 288.5&nbsp;eV correspond to the C─C, metal-O─C, and C═O bonds, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403736-fig-0003">3f</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0050_R_d89545366e925" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - ACS Nano">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403736-fig-0003">3g</a> displays the Se 3d spectrum, which consists of five main peaks: Se 3d<sub>5/2</sub> (54.2&nbsp;eV), Se 3d<sub>3/2</sub> (55.5&nbsp;eV), Co 3p<sub>3/2</sub> (58.4), Co 3p<sub>1/2</sub> (59.2), and Se-O<sub>x</sub> (58.9&nbsp;eV).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0051_R_d89545366e944" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Chem. Eng. J.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> XPS analyses of Co@NPC and Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC compounds which are fabricated from ZIF-12 are illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202403736-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d4675285-b1d6-4274-8430-0aab3a2210d1/smll202403736-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/d4675285-b1d6-4274-8430-0aab3a2210d1/smll202403736-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/10984f88-7358-4dfc-b997-a9f98be69aec/smll202403736-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d4675285-b1d6-4274-8430-0aab3a2210d1/smll202403736-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202403736-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202403736" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">SEM images of Cu@ZIF-12 a), Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC b), TEM images of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC c), Raman spectra of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC d), and FT-IR spectra of ZIF-12, Cu@ZIF-12, and Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC e).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Surface&nbsp;morphology of Cu@ZIF-12 and Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC was examined using scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403736-fig-0003">3a,b</a>, respectively. From SEM images of the Cu@ZIF-12 precursor, one can observe that the Cu@ZIF-12 shows homogeneous a rose-like structure with clearly distinguishable petals with sizes of ≈1–2 µm. SEM images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403736-fig-0003">3a</a>) confirm that the polyhedron morphology of the Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC sample is basically not preserved after the selenization/carbonization processes and it has evolved into a structure of small globules formed on a shapeless layer morphology with a size of ≈100–1000&nbsp;nm. Nevertheless, the Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC composite agglomerated into large inhomogeneous nanoparticles and cannot separated from the carbon matrix. SEM images of Co@NPC and Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC materials which are produced from ZIF-12 are imaged in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403736-fig-0003">3d</a> displays the Raman analysis of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC. Two distinct peaks were detected within the range of 500–2000 cm<sup>−1</sup>. Subsequently, peak fitting was conducted, leading to the identification of four prominent bands. The I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> ratio, which compares the intensity of the D band at 1344 cm<sup>−1</sup> to the G band at 1596 cm<sup>−1</sup>, is commonly employed to assess the crystallinity or level of defects in carbonaceous materials. When the I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> ratio is less than one, it typically indicates a higher level of graphitization and a lower level of disorder.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0052_R_d89545366e1068" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - J. Cleaner Prod.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> ratio was calculated as 1.17 for Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC which indicates that the degree of disorder is higher than degree of the graphitization.</p>
            
            <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403736-fig-0003">3e</a> displays the fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FT-IR) spectra of Cu@ZIF-12, showing a peak at 3353.2 cm<sup>−1</sup> labelled as -OH stretching vibrations of absorbed H<sub>2</sub>O molecules.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0053_R_d89545366e1094" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Nanoscale">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peaks at 1611.0, 1454.7, 1236.9, and 727.5 cm<sup>−1</sup> represent the distinctive vibrations of C═O, C═C, C─N, and Metal-N stretching, indicating the creation of ZIF-12 and Cu@ZIF-12. The highest intensities of C─H, C═O, C═C, C─N, and Metal-Se stretching vibrations in Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC are situated at 2933.0, 1738.7, 1526.1, 1110.0, and 670.9 cm<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0054_R_d89545366e1111" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Nano Res.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202403736-fig-0004">4a,c</a> shows that the Brunauer-Emmett-Teller&nbsp;(BET) surface areas of Cu@ZIF-12 and Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC are 61.8 and 386.6 m<sup>2</sup>g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption curves for Cu@ZIF-12 and Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC materials exhibit type of IV isotherms with H3–type and H4-type hysteresis loop, respectively, which confirmed the existence of the mesoporous materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0055_R_d89545366e1143" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Pure Appl. Chem.">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The H<sub>3</sub>-type hysteresis loop is observed with aggregates of plate-like particles giving rise to slit-shaped pores. The Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC material exhibits type of IV isotherms with H<sub>4</sub>–type hysteresis loop, associated with narrow slit-like pores, but in this case the Type I isotherm character is indicative of microporosity materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0055_R_d89545366e1159" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Pure Appl. Chem.">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The hysteresis loops are observed in the relative pressure ranges of 0.1–0.99 for Cu@ZIF-12 and 0.5–0.99 for Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC. The structure of the pores of the samples was analyzed using pore size distribution curves. According to Barret-Joyner-Halenda (BJH) graphics of Cu@ZIF-12 and Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC materials, adsorption average pore diameter (4&nbsp;V/A by BET) values are 7.8 and 4.9&nbsp;nm, respectively, suggesting the mesoporous structure of Cu@ZIF-12 and Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202403736-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/74027b5d-1bf7-4bd1-9f12-10e236e86c91/smll202403736-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/74027b5d-1bf7-4bd1-9f12-10e236e86c91/smll202403736-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/09ef05bf-45ae-4af1-ae6b-3bbdf58600b0/smll202403736-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/74027b5d-1bf7-4bd1-9f12-10e236e86c91/smll202403736-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202403736-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202403736" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherm and BJH graphics of Cu@ZIF-12 a,b), Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC c,d), energy-dispersive X-ray (EDX) spectra of Cu@ZIF-12 and Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC e).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The pore volumes are found as 0.24 cm<sup>3</sup>g<sup>−1</sup> for Cu@ZIF-12, and 0.532 cm<sup>3</sup>g<sup>−1</sup> for Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403736-fig-0004">4b,d</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). The pore diameters range from 5 to 15&nbsp;nm, confirming the mesoporous nature of Cu@ZIF-12 and Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC. The bigger specific surface area of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC facilitates interaction with the electrolyte, enhancing Na<sup>+</sup> and Li<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion during the electrochemical reaction and ultimately boosting battery performance. Furthermore, this design is beneficial for adapting to the changes in volume that occur during the charging and discharging of the cell.</p>
            
            <p>Also, the EDX spectrum was employed to understand the elemental distribution of Cu@ZIF-12 and Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202403736-fig-0005">5e</a> and <a href="#smll202403736-fig-0005">5e</a> inset). Notably, while Cu@ZIF-12 contains 52.16 wt.% C, 41.09 wt.% N, 1.82 wt.% Cu, and 4.93 wt.% Co elements, Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC contains 63.8 wt.% C, 5 wt.% N, 1.78 wt.% Cu, 4.35 wt.% Co, and 25.08 wt.% Se elements. These results confirm the related structure of the compounds has been successfully synthesized.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202403736-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c2b8eedc-8f20-4c91-8ddf-95e1417c4c78/smll202403736-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c2b8eedc-8f20-4c91-8ddf-95e1417c4c78/smll202403736-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/08b1b4ad-aec1-454f-8e05-41f48b8482c9/smll202403736-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c2b8eedc-8f20-4c91-8ddf-95e1417c4c78/smll202403736-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202403736-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202403736" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC for LIBs (All tests were performed in ethylene carbonate (EC)/diethyl carbonate (DEC)/dimethyl carbonate (DMC) with 5% FEC electrolyte); a) CV curves at four scan rates of 0.1 mVs<sup>−1</sup>; b) the galvanostatic charge/discharge profits of the Co@NPC, Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC, and Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC at 0.5 Ag<sup>−1</sup>; c) the rate performance at different specific currents; d) the cycling performance and coulombic efficiency at 2 Ag<sup>−1</sup>; e) EIS plots of the Co@NPC, Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC, and Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC devices in the frequency range from 0.01&nbsp;Hz to 100&nbsp;kHz at an open circuit voltage (The inset panels show the enlarged view of the plot at high frequencies).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202403736-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202403736-sec-0040-title">2.2 The Electrochemical Li<sup>+</sup> Storage Performances</h3>
            
            <p>The Li<sup>+</sup> storage performance of the electrodes was evaluated using CR2016 coin-type cells containing LiPF<sub>6</sub> electrolytes and lithium foils. The cycling performance of the initial four cycles is assessed by measuring them at a scanning rate of 0.1 mVs<sup>–1</sup> within a potential range of 0.01–3.0&nbsp;V versus Li<sup>+</sup>/Li, as seen in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403736-fig-0005">5a</a>. The disappearance of the cathodic peak at 0.69&nbsp;V in subsequent cycles is a result of the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layer forming on the Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0056_R_d89545366e1368" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - J. Mater. Chem. A">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The remaining CV curves exhibit strong agreement, suggesting excellent cycle stability and a highly reversible lithiation/delithiation process of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC combinations. Furthermore, the cathodic peaks at 0.71, 1.42, 1.65, and 1.78&nbsp;V represent that Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC has undergone four lithiation processes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0057_R_d89545366e1387" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - J. Power Sources">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The formation of these peaks is a result of the complex electrochemical process involving the reduction of both cobalt (Co) and copper (Cu). The anodic peaks at 1.86, 2.01, and 2.21&nbsp;V are attributed to the oxidation process of Co and Cu to Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC is conducted in three steps.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0057_R_d89545366e1398" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - J. Power Sources">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is important to note that the peaks in the CV curve coincide in the other cycles, showing the electrochemical activity is reversible.</p>
            
            <p>According to the galvanostatic charge-discharge profiles in 0.01–3.0&nbsp;V, the charge and discharge capacities of Co@NPC, Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC, and Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC at the 1st cycle were, 593, 615, and 983 mAhg<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 0.5 Ag<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. It is possible that the formation of a SEI layer during electrolyte degradation is responsible for the initially low Coulombic efficiency.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0058_R_d89545366e1422" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Mater. Today Adv.">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Among all prepared ZIF-derived materials, Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC exhibited the optimum Li<sup>+</sup> storage performance (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403736-fig-0005">5b</a>), and it delivered the specific capacities of 690, 547, 526, 472, 415, and 330 mAhg<sup>−1</sup> at the current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.3, 0.5, 1, and 2 Ag<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. When the current density was back to 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, a capacity of 763 mAhg<sup>−1</sup> was recovered (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403736-fig-0005">5c</a>). The cycling stability of the Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC electrode at a constant current rate at 2 Ag<sup>−1</sup> up to 750 cycles is illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403736-fig-0005">5d</a>. Despite 750 cycles of charge/discharge, the Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC electrode maintains a discharge capacity of 543 mAhg<sup>−1</sup>, indicating good cycling performance. The electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS) of the electrode materials are examined at room temperature to determine their impact on electrical conductivity, charge transport, and kinetic characteristics (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403736-fig-0005">5e</a>). The Nyquist plots of Co@NPC, Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC, and Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC displayed two unique components: a semicircle in the high-middle frequency region, which was caused by the creation of the SEI layer, and a sloping line in the low-frequency range, showing a diffusion-controlled process occurring in the electrode. The Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC material displayed a smaller semicircle region in the high-middle frequency when compared to Co@NPC and Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC. This suggests a decrease in the resistance to the transfer of charges and an improvement in the transport of lithium ions throughout the process of charging and discharging.</p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">Cyclic voltammetry was applied to examine the electrochemical kinetics of the internal components of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC at different rates, in order to gain a better understanding of its performance. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202403736-fig-0006">6a</a> displays the CV profiles of the Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC electrode at different scan speeds ranging from 0.1 to 1.0 mVs<sup>−1</sup>. Every charge-discharge profile consists of a single main reduction peak and a single oxidation peak that can be easily distinguished. The slight alteration in form with increasing scanning rate indicates that the reactions of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC are highly reversible. Previous research<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0059_R_d89545366e1527" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - ChemElectroChem">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> has shown a link between <i>i</i> and <i>v</i>, which is delineated in the following equations.

               <div class="inline-equation" id="smll202403736-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/ceab0dea-2afe-4297-abd3-2a6a0e469e25/smll202403736-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202403736:smll202403736-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/smll202403736-math-0001.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mi>i</mi>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                              <mi>a</mi>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi>v</mi>
                                 <mi>b</mi>
                              </msup>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}i = a{v^b}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
               <div class="inline-equation" id="smll202403736-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/ff661312-9a4c-41c7-9d2f-f4efafe7ee2c/smll202403736-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202403736:smll202403736-math-0002" display="block" location="graphic/smll202403736-math-0002.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mi>log</mi>
                                 <mspace width="0.28em"></mspace>
                              </mrow>
                              <mfenced close=")" open="(">
                                 <mi>i</mi>
                              </mfenced>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                              <mi>b</mi>
                              <mo linebreak="goodbreak">×</mo>
                              <mi>log</mi>
                              <mfenced close=")" open="(">
                                 <mi>v</mi>
                              </mfenced>
                              <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                              <mi>log</mi>
                              <mfenced close=")" open="(">
                                 <mi>a</mi>
                              </mfenced>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{\mathrm{log\;}}\left( i \right) = b \times \log \left( v \right) + \log \left( a \right)\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202403736-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4c56fd4e-affe-46d5-812c-af8cc22f46c7/smll202403736-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/4c56fd4e-affe-46d5-812c-af8cc22f46c7/smll202403736-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/02bca759-ef6f-49b0-90d1-58c910794986/smll202403736-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4c56fd4e-affe-46d5-812c-af8cc22f46c7/smll202403736-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202403736-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202403736" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves at six sweep rates; b) plots of log (peak current) versus log (scan rate) at different redox states; c) relative contribution of the capacitive and diffusion-controlled charge storage at different scan rates for Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC d) Galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) curves of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC apparent Li<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient and overpotential during lithiation/delithiation processes.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <div class="paragraph-element">The symbol “<i>i</i>” denotes the current (A), “<i>v</i>” corresponds to the scanning rate (Vs<sup>–1</sup>), and “<i>a</i>” and “<i>b</i>” are constants. The value of <i>b</i> plays a crucial role in determining the kinetics of materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0060_R_d89545366e1629" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Nano Res.">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As <i>b</i> approaches 0.5, the capacity storage mechanism is mostly influenced by a diffusion-controlled process. When the value of <i>b</i> approaches 1.0, the capacitive-controlled process becomes the dominant factor. The <i>b</i> values obtained from the slope of linear plots were 0.90 for cathodic peaks and 0.85 for anodic peaks, as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403736-fig-0006">6b</a>. In general, the value of <i>b</i> is directly correlated with the behaviour of energy storage. The <i>b</i> values ranging from 0.5 to 1.0 indicate a mixed mechanism where the capacitive-controlled process is predominant and accompanied by the diffusion-controlled process. Surface mechanisms primarily govern the incidence of electrochemical reactions. In addition, Equation&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#smll202403736-disp-0003" title="Link to equation">3</a> is used to obtain the capacitive contribution at different scanning rates<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0062_R_d89545366e1651" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - Rare Met.">62</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="smll202403736-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/2669e1bb-2be1-4beb-ad30-320f074382c1/smll202403736-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202403736:smll202403736-math-0003" display="block" location="graphic/smll202403736-math-0003.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mi>i</mi>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>k</mi>
                                 <mn>1</mn>
                              </msub>
                              <mi>v</mi>
                              <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>k</mi>
                                 <mn>2</mn>
                              </msub>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi>v</mi>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mn>1</mn>
                                    <mo>/</mo>
                                    <mn>2</mn>
                                 </mrow>
                              </msup>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}i = {k_1} v + {k_2}{v^{1/2}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>The terms <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> represent the corresponding contributions from the capacitive-controlled and diffusion-controlled factors. Furthermore, the capacitive contribution of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC gradually increased with increasing scan rates. The capacitive contribution ratios of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC were determined to be 56%, 64%, 72%, 76%, 78%, and 80% for scan rates of 0.1, 0.2, 0.4, 0.6, 0.8, and 1.0 mVs<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The nanomaterial structure exhibited an enhanced proportion and capacity of pseudocapacitance due to the increased morphology and porosity. The high-rate capacity and pseudocapacitive behaviour of the Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC nanoparticles may be attributed to their beneficial structural and compositional properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0061_R_d89545366e1717" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">In order to provide a more detailed explanation of the diffusion capability of the Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC anode, the researchers utilized the GITT shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403736-fig-0006">6d</a>. This approach was employed to determine the diffusion coefficient (D<sub>Li</sub><sup>+</sup>) of Li<sup>+</sup> ions within the active electrode at various voltages. The D<sub>Li+</sub> value was determined using Equation&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#smll202403736-disp-0004" title="Link to equation">4</a>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0062_R_d89545366e1744" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - Rare Met.">62</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="smll202403736-disp-0004"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/51574c9e-06ee-44a1-a65e-b8f527427340/smll202403736-math-0004.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202403736:smll202403736-math-0004" display="block" location="graphic/smll202403736-math-0004.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mi>D</mi>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                              <mfrac>
                                 <mn>4</mn>
                                 <mrow>

                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(4)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>The symbol τ represents the duration of the constant current pulse. <i>m<sub>B</sub></i>, <i>M<sub>B</sub></i>, and <i>V<sub>m</sub></i> refer to the mass, molar weight, and molar volume of the active material, respectively. S represents the cumulative surface area between the electrolyte and the electrode material. The D<sub>Li+</sub> values, determined according to the data shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403736-fig-0006">6d</a>, are graphed as a function of cell voltage. The quasi-equilibrium potential closely aligns with the discharge and charge curves, indicating that the processes of Li-ion insertion and extraction are kinetically favourable due to their strong ionic and electronic conductivities.</p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">In order to verify the mechanism of lithium-ion storage and the phase transition of the Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC anode, an in situ XRD analysis was conducted at a rate of 0.2 Ag<sup>−1</sup> during the first cycle (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202403736-fig-0007">7a</a>). We displayed the matching galvanostatic charge-discharge (GCD) profile on the left. The diffraction peaks seen at 45.6° (100), 50.7° (002), and 52.6° (101) in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403736-fig-0007">7a</a> are indicative of the conventional pattern of the Be window (COD no. 9012015). Furthermore, due to peak overlap with other substances, it is not possible to accurately distinguish the standard peaks of the metal Li. However, it is confirmed that Li metal was present during the whole charging and discharging process. Based on Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403736-fig-0007">7a</a>, the distinct peaks of Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub> (COD no. 9012804) and Cu<sub>2</sub>Se (COD no. 9015293) diminish progressively at angles of 26.65, 34.46, and 35.5°. Conversely, the distinct peaks of Li<sub>2</sub>Se (COD no. 1011373) gradually emerge. There wasn't much change in the XRD peaks during the charging process, which suggests that an amorphous Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC phase might form. These findings are in line with the CV curves.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0037_R_d89545366e1856" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The in situ XRD measurements demonstrated Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC reversible conversion process for lithium ion storage. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403736-fig-0007">7b</a> depicts the conversion process of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0063_R_d89545366e1878" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - Adv. Mater.">63</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> According to in situ XRD results and literature<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0064_R_d89545366e1883" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 64 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">64</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the Li-ion charge storage mechanism in Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC can be described as follows:

         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202403736-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll202403736-sec-0050-title">2.3 The Electrochemical Na<sup>+</sup> Storage Performances</h3>
            
            <p>The sodium storage properties of materials were investigated by assembling CR2016-type half cells. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202403736-fig-0008">8a</a> shows the CV curves of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC electrodes at a scan rate of 0.1 mVs<sup>−1</sup> in the potential ranging from 0.01 to 3.0&nbsp;V. Anode surface reactions and electrolyte degradation lead to the production of a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film, which is responsible for the sharp peak at 0.76&nbsp;V.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0065_R_d89545366e2045" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 65 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">65</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Following the first cathodic scan, four intensive cathodic peaks appeared at 1.93, 1.46, 1.15, 0.93, and 0.71&nbsp;V is suggested that the Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>/Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Cu<sub>1.8</sub>Se@NPC undergoes four sodiations steps during the discharge process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0065_R_d89545366e2059" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 65 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">65</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, we observe four distinct anodic peaks at 1.55, 1.78, 1.91, and 2.05&nbsp;V during the first anodic scan. These peaks correspond to the gradual transformation of metallic Co/Cu into Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>/Cu<sub>2</sub>Se. The reduction and oxidation peaks observed at 1.93 and 1.55&nbsp;V, respectively, are in excellent agreement with the identity peaks associated with Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0066" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0066_R_d89545366e2075" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 66 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">66</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The nearly identical behaviour of the CV curves of the second through fourth circles suggests that Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC is an electrode material with excellent cycling stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0063_R_d89545366e2087" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - Adv. Mater.">63</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll202403736-fig-0008"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5fc700e0-6a09-42a4-af49-0cdde1dcfc88/smll202403736-fig-0008-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/5fc700e0-6a09-42a4-af49-0cdde1dcfc88/smll202403736-fig-0008-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f0a1ae23-f2fb-4169-a96c-e9aff4edfa93/smll202403736-fig-0008-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5fc700e0-6a09-42a4-af49-0cdde1dcfc88/smll202403736-fig-0008-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 8<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202403736-fig-0008&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202403736" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC for SIBs (All tests were performed in 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaSO<sub>3</sub>CF<sub>3</sub> dissolved in diglyme); a) CV curves the Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC device at four scan rates of 0.1 mVs<sup>−1</sup>; b) cyclic performances the Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC, Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC, and Co@NPC devices at the current density of 0.1 Ag<sup>−1</sup>; c) Charge–discharge voltage profiles the Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC in the first five cycles at 0.1 Ag<sup>−1</sup>; d) the rate performance the Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC at different specific currents; e) the cycling performance and Coulombic efficiency the Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC at 2 Ag<sup>−1</sup>; f) Nyquist plots of the Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC device in the frequency range from 0.01&nbsp;Hz to 100&nbsp;kHz at an open circuit voltage (The inset panels show the enlarged view of the plot at high frequencies).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>In of all the materials generated from zeolitic imidazolate frameworks (ZIFs), Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC exhibited the optimum Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403736-fig-0008">8b</a>), and it delivered the specific capacities of 330, 318, 309, 298, 279, and 260 mAhg<sup>−1</sup> at the current densities of 0.02, 0.05, 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, and 1 Ag<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. When the current density was back to 0.01 A g<sup>−1</sup>, a capacity of 375 mAhg<sup>−1</sup> was recovered (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403736-fig-0008">8d</a>). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403736-fig-0008">8e</a> illustrates the cycling stability of the Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC electrode at a constant current rate of 2 Ag<sup>−1</sup> for 1000 cycles. The Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC demonstrates excellent cycling behaviour, maintaining a discharge capacity of 185 mAhg<sup>−1</sup> even after 1000 charge/discharge cycles. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was performed in order to analyze the factors behind the exceptional chemical performance of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC nanoparticles, as seen in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202403736-fig-0008">8f</a>. The Nyquist plots of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC nanomaterials showed an obvious R<sub>ct</sub> of 7, 41, and 292 Ω in NaSO<sub>3</sub>CF<sub>3</sub>-diglyme, the Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC, Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC, and Co@NPC materials, respectively. Comparatively, the Rct value of Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC indicates the composite's enhanced conductivity and efficient charge transfer during electrochemical processes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0067" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0067_R_d89545366e2263" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 67 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">67</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Cu<sub>2</sub>Se/Co<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub>@NPC nanomaterials demonstrated a small diameter of a semicircle and a short straight line in the low-frequency region, suggesting low resistance to charge transfer, high diffusivity of Na<sup>+</sup> ions, and excellent structural stability during cycling and rate performances for SIBs.</p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">The correlation between peak current (<i>i</i>) and scan rate (<i>v</i>) in CV measurements is as follows<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202403736-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202403736-bib-0059_R_d89545366e2284" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - ChemElectroChem">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="smll202403736-disp-0007"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->

                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(7)</span></div>
               <div class="inline-equation" id="smll202403736-disp-0008"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
